Divine Speech as Indication of the Wajib, Mustahil, and Ja'iz
Sanusi defines the relation of divine speech as indication and informing for the three categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine speech relates to the three rational categories through indication and informing, and that this relation is determinate and eternal.

Core Idea
Divine speech indicates the necessary, possible, and impossible as an eternal, determinate relation.

Sanusi begins by explaining that Allah's speech does not merely create or command, but also informs. He shows how the Qur'an itself indicates what is obligatory, possible, and impossible for Allah. This establishes that the relation of divine speech to these categories is one of indication, not of temporal enactment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Focus on understanding the key claim that speech relates to the three categories as indication and informing.
2
Identify the three examples
The passage gives Qur'anic examples for each category. Find which verse corresponds to wajib, which to ja'iz, and which to mustahil. Notice that each verse illustrates a different type of content.
3
Understand 'tanziji qadim'
The passage concludes that this indicating relation is determinate (tanziji) and eternal (qadim). Reflect on why it is important that this relation is not temporal.
This phrase is key to the Ash'ari view of the eternity of divine speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتعلق الكلام بالواجبات والمستحيلات والجائزات تعلّق دلالة وإخبار. مثال دلالة الكلام على الواجب قوله تعالى: ﴿ قُلْ هُوَ اللَّهُ أَحَدٌ ﴾ (الإخلاص: 1) إلى آخر السورة، ومثال دلالته على الجائز قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَرَبُّكَ يَخْلُقُ مَا يَشَاءُ وَيَخْتَارُ ﴾ (القصص: 68)، ومثال دلالته على المستحيل قوله تعالى: ﴿ لَوْ كَانَ فِيهِمَا آلِهَةً إِلَّا اللَّهُ لَفَسَدَتَا ﴾ (الأنبياء: 22). وتعلق الكلام بالواجب والمستحيل والجائز، بمعنى دلالته عليه، تنجيزي قديم. وأما الأمر والنهي فإن لم يقل برجوعهما إلى الخبر من حيث المعنى، فلهما تعلقان بأفعال المكلفين صلوحي قديم قبل وجودهم، وتنجيزي حادث بعد وجودهم.
And speech relates to the obligatory, the impossible, and the possible with a relation of indication and informing. Example of speech's indication of the obligatory is His statement, the Exalted: 'Say, He is Allah, the One' (al-Ikhlas: 1) to the end of the sura; example of its indication of the possible is His statement: 'And your Lord creates what He wills and chooses' (al-Qasas: 68); example of its indication of the impossible is His statement: 'If there had been in them [the heavens and earth] gods besides Allah, they would have been ruined' (al-Anbiya: 22). And the relation of speech to the obligatory, the impossible, and the possible, meaning its indication of them, is determinate, eternal. As for command and prohibition, if one does not say that they reduce to information in meaning, then they have two relations to the acts of the obligated persons: a potential relation, eternal before their existence, and a determinate relation, originated after their existence.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage defines the relationship of divine speech (al-kalam) to the three categories of things: wajib (obligatory/necessary), mustahil (impossible), and ja'iz (possible). The author explains that speech relates to them as indication (dalala) and informing (ikhbar), not as creating or commanding in this context. He then provides Qur'anic examples for each: for the wajib, Surat al-Ikhlas; for the ja'iz, the verse about Allah creating what He wills; for the mustahil, the verse about gods besides Allah causing ruin. Then he distinguishes two kinds of relation: tanziji (determinate/actual) and saluhi (potential). The relation of speech to the three categories is tanziji and eternal (qadim). For command (amr) and prohibition (nahy), if they are not reduced to information (khabar) in meaning, then they have two relations to human actions: a potential relation that is eternal before humans exist, and a determinate relation that is originated after they exist. This distinction preserves the eternity of the attribute of speech while accounting for its relation to temporal acts.

Key Terms
كلام
دلالة
إخبار
تعلّق تنجيزي
واجب
مستحيل
جائز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the nature of the relation of divine speech to the three categories?
A) Creation
B) Command
C) Indication and informing
D) Potential

Key Takeaway
Divine speech indicates what is necessary, possible, and impossible for Allah through an eternal, determinate relation of informing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the eternal, determinate indication of divine speech challenge your tendency to prioritize your own opinions or desires over what the Qur'an declares necessary or impossible? What specific area of your life are you most resistant to letting the Qur'an define what is possible for Allah?
?
When you recite Surah al-Ikhlas, do you truly settle in your heart that Allah's oneness is an absolute necessity, or do you allow mental reservations that entertain other possibilities? What would it mean for your worship if you fully internalized that this is an eternal indication?
?
If divine speech's indication is eternal, then your response to it today is not a separate act but a part of your eternal relationship with Allah. Are you living as though your current beliefs and actions are being weighed against an unchanging divine declaration? What needs to change in your daily choices to align with what Allah's speech has eternally deemed necessary, possible, and impossible?

The Two Relations of Command and Prohibition
Sanusi explains the dual relation of command and prohibition to human acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine command and prohibition have two relations to human acts: an eternal potential relation and a temporal determinate relation.

Core Idea
Command and prohibition relate to acts both eternally in potential and temporally when the act occurs.

Sanusi now addresses a subtle point: commands and prohibitions in the Qur'an are not merely informative. They relate to human actions in two distinct ways. One relation is eternal and potential, existing before humans are created; the other is temporal and actual, occurring when the act is performed. This distinction preserves the eternity of Allah's speech while accounting for its connection to temporal events.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Focus on the sentence about command and prohibition. Read the Arabic and translation carefully.
2
Understand the condition
The passage begins with 'if one does not say that they reduce to information in meaning'. Consider why this condition is important. If command and prohibition are essentially informative, the earlier analysis suffices. If not, a different analysis is needed.
3
Distinguish the two relations
Identify the two relations: 'saluhi qadim' (potential, eternal) and 'tanziji hadith' (determinate, originated). Reflect on how both can coexist without contradiction.
The potential relation exists before the act; the determinate relation arises when the act is performed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And speech relates to the obligatory, the impossible, and the possible with a relation of indication and informing. Example of speech's indication of the obligatory is His statement, the Exalted: 'Say, He is Allah, the One' (al-Ikhlas: 1) to the end of the sura; example of its indication of the possible is His statement: 'And your Lord creates what He wills and chooses' (al-Qasas: 68); example of its indication of the impossible is His statement: 'If there had been in them [the heavens and earth] gods besides Allah, they would have been ruined' (al-Anbiya: 22). Commentary
This passage defines the relationship of divine speech (al-kalam) to the three categories of things: wajib (obligatory/necessary), mustahil (impossible), and ja'iz (possible). The author explains that speech relates to them as indication (dalala) and informing (ikhbar), not as creating or commanding in this context. He then provides Qur'anic examples for each: for the wajib, Surat al-Ikhlas; for the ja'iz, the verse about Allah creating what He wills; for the mustahil, the verse about gods besides Allah causing ruin. Then he distinguishes two kinds of relation: tanziji (determinate/actual) and saluhi (potential). The relation of speech to the three categories is tanziji and eternal (qadim). For command (amr) and prohibition (nahy), if they are not reduced to information (khabar) in meaning, then they have two relations to human actions: a potential relation that is eternal before humans exist, and a determinate relation that is originated after they exist. This distinction preserves the eternity of the attribute of speech while accounting for its relation to temporal acts.

Key Terms
أمر
نهي
تعلّق صلوحي
تعلّق تنجيزي
قديم
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Allah's command only exists when the act is performed, making it temporal.
Correction: The passage shows that there is an eternal potential relation before the act exists.

Checkpoint
What two relations do command and prohibition have to the acts of the obligated persons?
A) One relation only: temporal
B) Potential eternal and determinate temporal
C) Informative only
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
Commands and prohibitions in divine speech relate to human acts through an eternal potential relation and a temporal determinate relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe that Allah's command to you is eternal—it has always been there, even before you were born. Do you live your life as if you are responding to an eternal call, or do you act as if the command only matters when you happen to think about it? How would you have to change if you took the eternal potential relation seriously?
?
When you sin, does it help you to know that the prohibition against that sin had an eternal relation to your act? Does that make you feel more accountable, or does it make you feel trapped? In either case, what does this doctrine demand of you right now—not in theory, but in your next moment of temptation?
?
If the determinate relation of command occurs exactly when you act, then each act of obedience or disobedience is a moment of direct connection with Allah's speech. How would your daily routine change if you saw every action as a place where Allah's command concretely applies to you? Can you point to one specific area of your life where you habitually ignore that connection?

Scriptural Proof for Divine Speech
Sanusi cites Qur'an and hadith as proofs for divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural proofs from the Qur'an and Sunnah that establish the attribute of speech for Allah.

Core Idea
Allah's speech is proven by the Qur'an and the Prophet's hadith.

Sanusi provides the scriptural foundation for the divine attribute of speech. He cites two primary texts: a Qur'anic verse describing Allah's direct speech to Musa, and a prophetic hadith about Allah speaking to every believer on the Day of Resurrection. These texts are the starting point for the Sunni belief that Allah speaks.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the meaning.
Notice the concise style: each proof is introduced with 'From the Book...' and 'From the Sunnah...'.
2
Identify the two proofs
Locate the Qur'anic verse and the hadith. Understand what each text asserts about divine speech.
3
Reflect on the role of scripture
Ask yourself: why does the author start with scriptural proof rather than intellectual argument? This shows the priority of revelation in Sunni theology.
This connects to the broader methodology of Ahl al-Sunna.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودليل وجوب الكلام من الكتاب قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَكَلَّمَ اللَّهُ مُوسَى تَكْلِيمًا ﴾ (النساء: 164)، ومن السنة قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «ما منكم من أحد إلا وسيكلمه ربه يوم القيامة ليس بينه وبينه ترجمان».
And the proof of the necessity of speech from the Book is His statement, the Exalted: 'And Allah spoke to Moses with [direct] speech' (al-Nisa: 164); and from the Sunnah is his statement, may Allah bless him and grant him peace: 'There is none of you but his Lord will speak to him on the Day of Resurrection, there being no interpreter between Him and him.'
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage provides the scriptural proof (dalil) for the necessity (wujub) of the divine attribute of speech. It cites the Qur'anic verse where Allah says He spoke directly to Moses, and the hadith where the Prophet says every believer will be spoken to by Allah on the Day of Resurrection without an interpreter. These texts establish that Allah is indeed a speaker (mutakallim). The proof from the Qur'an and Sunnah is sufficient for the Ahl al-Sunna, as the intellectual proof will be discussed later.

Key Terms
وجوب الكلام (wujub al-kalam)
دليل (dalil)
كتاب (kitab)
سنة (sunna)
تكليم (taklim)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think intellectual proof is primary and scripture is secondary.
Correction: Sanusi begins with scriptural proof because for Ahl al-Sunna, revelation is the primary source of theological knowledge. Intellectual proof is important but comes after scripture.

Checkpoint
What is the proof given for the necessity of divine speech from the Sunnah?
A) The Prophet said Allah spoke to Musa.
B) The hadith that each person will be spoken to by Allah on the Day of Resurrection.
C) The consensus of the scholars.
D) The rational argument from perfection.

Key Takeaway
Allah's speech is a necessary attribute proven directly by the Qur'an and the authentic Sunnah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Consensus on Eternal Speech
Author states the consensus of Ahl al-Sunna on the eternity of divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Ahl al-Sunna are unanimous in affirming that Allah's speech is eternal and pre-eternal, and that this consensus is foundational.

Core Idea
The consensus of Ahl al-Sunna establishes Allah's speech as eternal, not created.

The author begins by stating a foundational consensus: all Ahl al-Sunna agree that Allah speaks with an eternal speech. This consensus is not merely a historical fact; it sets the stage for the discussion of divine attributes. The passage also hints that the intellectual proof will follow, but first we need to recognize this entrenched agreement.

Guided Path
1
Read the consensus statement
Read the opening Arabic sentence carefully: 'وأجمعت أهل السنة على أن الله تعالى متكلم بكلام قديم أزلي. وبرهان العقل فسيأتي.' Notice the strength of the term 'أجمعت'.
2
Understand the significance of ijma'
Recognize that this is a claim of unanimous agreement (ijma') among Sunni scholars, which gives the belief a strong foundation in Islamic legal and theological methodology.
Ijmāʿ is a primary source of Islamic knowledge.
3
Prepare for the proof
Note the author's statement 'وبرهان العقل فسيأتي' – the intellectual proof is coming, but the consensus itself is sufficient for the believer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجمعت أهل السنة على أن الله تعالى متكلم بكلام قديم أزلي. وبرهان العقل فسيأتي.
قال الصاوي إن دليل الكلام نقلي وكذلك السمع والبصر، لأن صنع العالم لا يتوقف على الاتصاف بها. يقصد من هذا أننا لا نعرف بالعقل من صفات الله إلا ما كان مدلولا من النظر في العالم. ثم قال إن قلت إنه يمكن أن يكون دليلها عقلياً وتقريره أن تقول لو لم يتصف بها لاتصف بضدها وهو نقص. والنقص عليه محال. أجيب بأن النقص مشاهد في الحوادث ولا يقاس القديم على الحادث، لأن كل كمال في حق الحادث لا يلزم أن يكون كمالاً في حق الله، ألا ترى أن الزوجة والولد فإنهما كمال في حق الحادث لا القديم. فضعف الدليل العقلي. من هذا أن العقل لا يستطيع أن يتوصل إلى الاستدلال على السمع والبصر والكلام من مجرد ما يملكه من معلومات عن العالم من حيث حدوثه، وأن الاستدلال على وجوب الاتصاف بها بالقول بأن الله إن لم يتصف بها لزم الاتصاف بأضدادها، وأضدادها نقص وهي العمى والصمم والخرس، فيلزم وجوب اتصافه تعالى بها. فإن هذا الاستدلال ليس قوياً، لأنه ليس كل ما كان كمالاً في حق الحادث يلزم أن يكون كمالا في حق القديم. وكذلك فإذا كان عدم اتصاف الحادث بالبصر يلزم منه اتصافه بالعمى، فلا يدل هذا على أن الله إذا لم يكن موصوفا بالبصر فإنه موصوف بالعمى! لإمكان أن يكون البصر أصلاً ليس كمالاً محضاً وبالتالي فلا يجب اتصاف الله تعالى به فتأمل.
And the Ahl al-Sunna are unanimous that Allah, the Exalted, is speaking with an eternal, pre-eternal speech. And the intellectual proof will come later.
Al-Sawi said: The proof of speech is transmitted, and likewise hearing and sight, because the fashioning of the world does not depend on being qualified by them. By this it is meant that we do not know by the intellect from the attributes of Allah except what is indicated by consideration of the world. Then he said: If you say that their proof could be intellectual, and its formulation is that you say: If He were not qualified by them, He would be qualified by their opposites, which is deficiency, and deficiency is impossible for Him. The answer is that deficiency is observed in originated things, and the Eternal is not measured against the originated, because every perfection in the case of the originated does not necessarily entail that it is perfection in the case of Allah. Do you not see that a spouse and a child are perfection in the case of the originated, not the Eternal. So the intellectual proof is weak. From this [it is clear] that the intellect cannot arrive at inference of hearing, sight, and speech solely from the information it possesses about the world in terms of its origination, and that the inference of the necessity of being qualified by them by saying that if Allah were not qualified by them, He would be qualified by their opposites—deafness, blindness, and muteness—would entail the necessity of His being qualified by them. Indeed, this inference is not strong, because not everything that is perfection in the case of the originated necessarily entails that it is perfection in the case of the Eternal. Likewise, if the absence of sight in the originated entails its being blind, this does not indicate that when Allah is not qualified by sight, He is qualified by blindness! For it is possible that sight is not, in the first place, a pure perfection, and consequently it is not necessary for Allah, the Exalted, to be qualified by it. So reflect.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage first states the consensus (ijma') of Ahl al-Sunna that Allah is speaking with an eternal, pre-eternal speech (kalam qadim azali). Then it mentions that the intellectual proof will follow. The footnote by al-Sawi then explains that the proof for speech (like sight and hearing) is primarily transmitted (naqli), not intellectual, because the creation of the world does not depend on these attributes. He argues that one cannot use a purely intellectual proof based on the argument from perfection and deficiency, because what is perfection for created beings may not be perfection for the Eternal. For example, having a spouse and child is perfection for a human but not for Allah. Therefore, the intellectual proof (that if Allah lacked speech, He would have its opposite, muteness, which is deficiency) is weak. The footnote elaborates that the inference from deficiency in creatures to deficiency in the Creator is invalid, as sight itself might not be a pure perfection. Thus, the only certain proofs for these attributes are scriptural.

Key Terms
أهل السنة
إجماع
كلام قديم
كلام أزلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Allah's speech is created or that the Quran is a temporal event.
Correction: The consensus of Ahl al-Sunna affirms that Allah's speech is eternal, not created, and that the Quran is His uncreated speech.

Checkpoint
What does the consensus (ijma') in this passage specifically affirm about Allah's speech?
A) It is created.
B) It is eternal.
C) It is only metaphorical.
D) It is the same as human speech.

Key Takeaway
The Ahl al-Sunna unanimously affirm that Allah's speech is eternal and pre-eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you truly believe these are the very words of Allah that have existed eternally with Him? How does that reality change your focus during prayer?
?
The passage confirms the consensus of Ahl al-Sunna. Have you ever examined your own heart to see if you submit to this consensus, or do you harbor doubts influenced by modern critiques? What will you do to strengthen your certainty?
?
The author promises an intellectual proof to follow. Are you content to accept truth only by authority, or do you also seek intellectual understanding? What steps will you take to use your reason in affirming your faith without falling into doubt?

Weakness of Intellectual Proof for Divine Attributes
Al-Sawi argues that intellectual proof for divine hearing, sight, and speech is weak due to the invalidity of comparing the Eternal to the originated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why intellectual proof alone is insufficient to establish certain divine attributes like speech, hearing, and sight, and why the argument from perfection and deficiency is weak.

Core Idea
What is perfect for creation may not be perfection for the Creator.

In a footnote, al-Sawi provides a subtle methodological critique. While consensus and transmitted proofs establish divine attributes, some have attempted to prove them through pure reason. Al-Sawi shows that the intellectual argument that God must possess speech, hearing, and sight (because otherwise He would have their opposites, which are deficiencies) is not sound. The reason is that we cannot simply transfer what is considered perfection in created beings to the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Sawi's footnote
Read the Arabic passage from 'قال الصاوي' to the end. Notice the structure: a statement that the proof is transmitted, then an objection and answer.
The footnote is a continuous argument.
2
Understand the objection
The objection claims that if Allah lacked these attributes, He would have their opposites (deafness, blindness, muteness), which are deficiencies. Since deficiency is impossible for Allah, He must have the attributes.
This is an argument by contradiction.
3
Analyze the refutation
Al-Sawi argues that the analogy between the created and the Eternal is invalid. What is a perfection in a creature (like having a spouse) is not a perfection for Allah. Similarly, sight might not be a pure perfection; thus the intellectual proof fails.
The key phrase: 'لا يقاس القديم على الحادث'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجمعت أهل السنة على أن الله تعالى متكلم بكلام قديم أزلي. وبرهان العقل فسيأتي.
قال الصاوي إن دليل الكلام نقلي وكذلك السمع والبصر، لأن صنع العالم لا يتوقف على الاتصاف بها. يقصد من هذا أننا لا نعرف بالعقل من صفات الله إلا ما كان مدلولا من النظر في العالم. ثم قال إن قلت إنه يمكن أن يكون دليلها عقلياً وتقريره أن تقول لو لم يتصف بها لاتصف بضدها وهو نقص. والنقص عليه محال. أجيب بأن النقص مشاهد في الحوادث ولا يقاس القديم على الحادث، لأن كل كمال في حق الحادث لا يلزم أن يكون كمالاً في حق الله، ألا ترى أن الزوجة والولد فإنهما كمال في حق الحادث لا القديم. فضعف الدليل العقلي. من هذا أن العقل لا يستطيع أن يتوصل إلى الاستدلال على السمع والبصر والكلام من مجرد ما يملكه من معلومات عن العالم من حيث حدوثه، وأن الاستدلال على وجوب الاتصاف بها بالقول بأن الله إن لم يتصف بها لزم الاتصاف بأضدادها، وأضدادها نقص وهي العمى والصمم والخرس، فيلزم وجوب اتصافه تعالى بها. فإن هذا الاستدلال ليس قوياً، لأنه ليس كل ما كان كمالاً في حق الحادث يلزم أن يكون كمالا في حق القديم. وكذلك فإذا كان عدم اتصاف الحادث بالبصر يلزم منه اتصافه بالعمى، فلا يدل هذا على أن الله إذا لم يكن موصوفا بالبصر فإنه موصوف بالعمى! لإمكان أن يكون البصر أصلاً ليس كمالاً محضاً وبالتالي فلا يجب اتصاف الله تعالى به فتأمل.
And the Ahl al-Sunna are unanimous that Allah, the Exalted, is speaking with an eternal, pre-eternal speech. And the intellectual proof will come later.

By this it is meant that we do not know by the intellect from the attributes of Allah except what is indicated by consideration of the world. Then he said: If you say that their proof could be intellectual, and its formulation is that you say: If He were not qualified by them, He would be qualified by their opposites, which is deficiency, and deficiency is impossible for Him. Likewise, if the absence of sight in the originated entails its being blind, this does not indicate that when Allah is not qualified by sight, He is qualified by blindness! Take a moment to repent for any human-like image you have formed of Allah and ask Him to teach you His names as He deserves.
?
When you think about Allah's attribute of 'hearing,' do you automatically think of a physical ear? This lesson warns against measuring the Eternal by the originated. What practical step can you take today to purify your understanding of Allah from any trace of anthropomorphism? Will you commit to reading a book on tawhid or asking a scholar about the correct understanding of Allah's attributes?
?
Your intellect is a gift, but this lesson shows it has limits in knowing Allah's attributes. Are you willing to accept that your own reasoning can lead you astray in matters of creed, and that you must submit to revelation even when your mind finds it difficult? How Musa Heard Allah's Eternal Speech
Sanusi explains how Musa heard Allah's eternal speech by removal of obstacle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Allah's eternal speech was heard by Prophet Musa according to the Ash'ari school, and why this does not imply change in the divine attribute.

Core Idea
Allah's speech is eternal; Musa heard it by removal of an obstacle, not by new creation.

This passage tackles a question about the event of Allah speaking to Musa. How can an eternal attribute be heard at a specific moment? Sanusi explains using the concept of 'removing the obstacle' (mani'). This approach protects divine immutability while affirming the scriptural event.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Let Sanusi's concise wording sink in.
Notice that the passage begins with 'ملاحظة' (note), indicating a clarification.
2
Identify the core mechanism
Find the phrase 'أزال منه المانع' (removed from him the obstacle). This is the key: the obstacle is what prevents us from hearing the eternal speech normally.
The speech itself is not created; it is 'قديم' (eternal) and not composed of letters or sound.
3
Contrast with temporal speech
Sanusi explicitly denies that Allah initiated speech at that moment or that speech ceased afterward. Why? Because the eternal does not change.
This safeguards the attribute of speech from temporal alteration.
4
Relate to the Ash'ari position
Sanusi attributes this view to Imam al-Ash'ari and most Ahl al-Sunna. Understand that this is a standard Ash'ari defense of divine immutability.
5
Reflect on the misconception
The common mistake is to think Allah created a new speech for Musa. Consider how the 'obstacle removal' model avoids that error.
This shows how kalam reconciles scriptural events with rational principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ملاحظة: اعلم أن كلام الله تعالى للنبي موسى عليه السلام أنه أزال منه المانع كلامه القديم الذي ليس بحرف ولا صوت على مذهب الإمام الأشعري وأكثر أهل السنة، ثم رد عليه المانع فلم يسمع، وليس أن الله تعالى ابتدأ كلامه لموسى ولا انعدام كلامه بعد رد المانع عليه لأن كلامه تعالى قديم والقديم لا يتغير ولا يتبدل.
Note: Know that the speech of Allah, the Exalted, to the Prophet Musa, peace be upon him, is that He removed from him the obstacle [such that] His eternal speech, which is not letters or sound, [was heard], according to the school of Imam al-Ash'ari and most of the Ahl al-Sunna; then He returned the obstacle to him, so he did not hear; and it is not that Allah, the Exalted, initiated His speech to Musa nor that His speech ceased after returning the obstacle to him, because His speech, the Exalted, is eternal, and the eternal does not change nor alter.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This note explains the modality of Allah's speech to Prophet Musa. According to Imam al-Ash'ari and most Ahl al-Sunna, Allah removed the obstacle (al-mani') that normally prevents humans from hearing the eternal speech (which is not composed of letters or sound), so Musa heard it. Then the obstacle was returned, so he did not continue hearing. This does not imply that Allah began His speech at that moment or that His speech ceased, because His speech is eternal and does not change. The language of 'removing the obstacle' avoids temporalizing the eternal attribute.

Key Terms
مانع (mani')
كلام نفسي (kalam nafsi)
حرف (harf)
صوت (sawt)
قديم (qadim)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Allah created a new speech (letters and sounds) for Musa at the moment of communication.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the speech is eternal and not composed of letters or sound; the hearing occurred through removal of an obstacle, not creation of a new speech.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari perspective in this passage, how did Prophet Musa hear the speech of Allah?
A) Allah created a voice and letters for Musa.
B) Allah removed the obstacle preventing him from hearing the eternal speech.
C) Allah revealed it through an angel.
D) Musa heard it in a dream.

Key Takeaway
Prophet Musa heard Allah's eternal, non-physical speech through the removal of an obstacle, not through any change or temporalization of the divine attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Allah speaking to Musa, do you unconsciously picture a voice with letters and sounds? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what does it mean for your understanding of Allah's eternity? Will you intentionally remind yourself that Allah's speech is beyond physical limitations?
?
Sanusi describes an 'obstacle' that was removed for Musa. What obstacles in your own life—distractions, desires, or doubts—prevent you from truly perceiving Allah's eternal truths? What would it take to have that obstacle removed, and are you willing to pursue that?
?
The eternal does not change. Yet your feelings of connection to Allah fluctuate. Does this truth comfort you or disturb you? Are you placing your certainty in Allah's unchanging attributes, or in your own changing perceptions? Commit to one practice this week that grounds you in the Unchanging One.

The Seven Demands of the Attributes of Meanings
Sanusi enumerates the seven demands of the attributes of meanings, except life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven rational demands that apply to the attributes of meanings and why the attribute of life is an exception.

Core Idea
Seven demands define the attributes of meanings—but life has six.

In Ash'ari theology, the attributes of meanings (like power, will, and knowledge) are described by a set of seven rational demands. These demands ensure the attributes are necessary and perfect. However, the attribute of life stands apart with only six demands. This note from Sanusi introduces this classification and its single exception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Notice the list of seven demands and the exception at the end.
The passage is concise; every word matters.
2
Identify each demand
Go through the seven demands one by one: existence, pre-eternity, permanence, being different from the essence, self-sufficiency from a specifier, unity, and universality of relation.
These are the necessary properties of the attributes.
3
Understand the exception for life
Note that life has only six demands, lacking 'universality of relation'. Think about why life might not relate to multiple objects in the same way.
This exception is a key point in Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ملاحظة: صفات المعاني لها مطالب سبعة: وجودها وقدمها وبقاؤها ومخالفتها وغناؤها عن المخصص ووحدتها وعموم تعلقها بما تتعلق به إلا الحياة فمطالبها ستة.
Note: The attributes of meanings have seven demands: their existence, their pre-eternity, their permanence, their being different [from the essence], their being self-sufficient from the specifier, their unity, and the universality of their relation to what they relate to; except for life, its demands are six.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This note enumerates the seven demands (matalib) of the attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani) — the attributes that subsist in the essence like power, will, knowledge, etc. The seven are: existence (wujud), pre-eternity (qidam), permanence (baqa'), being different from the essence (mukhalafa), self-sufficiency from a specifier (ghina 'an al-mukhasşis), unity (wahda), and universality of relation ('umum ta'alliq) to all their objects. However, the attribute of life (al-hayat) has only six demands, lacking the last one because life does not relate to multiple objects in the same way. This classification is central to Ash'ari theology for describing these attributes.

حياة (hayat)

Correction: The passage explicitly states life has six demands, lacking universality of relation.

Attributes of Meanings: Neither Identical nor Separate
Sanusi explains the correct position on attributes of meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attributes of meanings are said to be neither identical to Allah's essence nor separate from it.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are like one with ten: neither the same nor separate.

This short but crucial note from Sanusi clarifies a subtle point about the divine attributes of meanings. Often, thinkers fall into extremes – either saying the attributes are exactly the essence or that they are distinct entities. Sanusi rejects both, using a simple mathematical analogy to illustrate the correct middle path.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the original Arabic and its literal translation carefully. Notice the key terms 'عَين' and 'غَير'.
The passage uses a very direct style: 'اعلم أن...' – know that.
2
Understand the analogy
The analogy of the number one and the number ten is key. One is not identical to ten, nor is it a separate entity from ten. It is related in a unique way. Try to grasp how this applies to the divine essence and its attributes.
The analogy is not perfect, but it illustrates the concept of 'non-identity and non-separation'.
3
Examine why 'identity' is rejected
Sanusi says 'ayniyya (identity) is forbidden because it implies union (ittihad), which is impossible for the divine. Reflect on why union would be impossible.
Union would mean the attribute and essence become one in a way that compromises divine simplicity.
4
Examine why 'otherness' is rejected
Otherness in the sense of separation (infisal) is also forbidden because it would imply that the attributes are originated (hadith). Think about why separation implies origination.
If attributes were separate entities, they would be contingent and created, which is not befitting of Allah.
5
Synthesize the correct position
Now understand the balanced position: attributes are neither identical nor separate. They are real and eternal, yet not distinct from the essence in a way that implies multiplicity or change.
This is a classic Ash'ari position to preserve both divine simplicity and the reality of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ملاحظة: اعلم أن صفات المعاني لا يقال فيها عين الذات ولا غير الذات. فهي كالواحد مع العشرة لا عين العشرة ولا غيرها. فالعينية ممنوعة لفظاً واعتقاداً لأن الاتحاد محال والغيرية بمعنى الانفصال عن الذات فلا تجوز لفظاً ولا اعتقاداً لأنه يلزم عليها الحدوث.
Note: Know that the attributes of meanings are not said to be identical to the essence nor other than the essence. They are like the one with ten: neither identical to ten nor other than it. So identity is forbidden in wording and belief because union is impossible, and otherness in the sense of separation from the essence is not permissible in wording or belief because it would necessitate origination.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This note clarifies the correct position regarding the relationship between the attributes of meanings and the divine essence. One should not say they are identical ('ayn) to the essence, because that would imply union (ittihad), which is impossible. Nor should one say they are other (ghayr) in the sense of being separate (infisal) from the essence, because that would imply that they are originated (hadith) and separate entities. Instead, the relationship is like that of the number one to the number ten: it is neither the same as ten nor separate from it. The correct position is to avoid both 'ayniyya and ghayriyya in wording and belief, affirming that the attributes are neither identical to nor distinct from the essence in a way that implies separation.

Key Terms
عينية ('ayniyya)
غيرية (ghayriyya)
اتحاد (ittihad)
انفصال (infisal)
حدوث (huduth)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that if attributes are not identical to the essence, they must be separate entities.
Correction: The passage clarifies that they are neither identical nor separate; they are like the number one with ten.

Checkpoint
What is the correct position regarding the relation of attributes of meanings to the divine essence?
A) They are identical to the essence.
B) They are separate from the essence.
C) Neither identical nor separate in a way that implies separation.
D) They are created.

Key Takeaway
The attributes of meanings are neither identical to Allah's essence nor separate from it; they are like the number one in relation to ten.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah's mercy or power, do you instinctively imagine them as 'parts' of Him or as separate entities? This lesson tells you that such a mental image is forbidden. How can you retrain your mind to conceive of His attributes as truly Him without reducing them to parts or separate things? What specific step will you take this week to correct that mental image?
?
The analogy of one and ten is used to help us understand a difficult concept. Reflect on a time when you struggled to hold two truths together in your faith (e.g., mercy and justice). How does this lesson's principle help you avoid falling into extremes? What will you do the next time you face a seeming contradiction in Allah's attributes?
?
Sanusi says calling the attributes 'other' would lead to the error of origination. Have you ever, even unknowingly, acted as though one of Allah's attributes is temporary or conditional? For example, do you ever doubt that His mercy is always present? This lesson demands that you affirm all attributes as eternal and inseparable from His essence. What change in your daily worship or du'a does this require?

Eternal Speech and Moses' Hearing: Ahl al-Sunnah Consensus
States the Ahl al-Sunnah consensus on eternal speech and explains Moses' hearing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ahl al-Sunnah consensus that Allah's speech is eternal and beginningless, and how Moses' hearing of that speech is explained without attributing change or temporality to Allah.

Core Idea
Allah's speech is eternal and unchanging; Moses heard it by removal of a barrier, not by a change in God.

This passage affirms the consensus of Ahl al-Sunnah on the eternal nature of Allah's speech. It then addresses a potential misconception about the event of Moses hearing Allah: that it involved a beginning or end of speech. The author explains that it was a removal of the barrier (māni‘), not an initiation or cessation of divine speech.

Guided Path
1
Understand the consensus
Read the opening line stating that Ahl al-Sunnah unanimously agree Allah speaks with eternal speech. Note the promise of a rational proof later.
This consensus is foundational for the attribute of speech.
2
Grasp the key clarification
Focus on the note about Moses. It says God removed a barrier so Moses heard His eternal speech, which is neither letter nor sound according to Ash‘arī school.
The barrier concept is crucial: it prevents hearing, not speech.
3
Discern what is not the case
The passage denies that God initiated speech for Moses or that speech ceased after. That would imply change, which is impossible for the eternal.
Eternal cannot begin or end.
4
Connect to the eternal attribute
Reflect on the closing sentence: the eternal does not change or alter. This is the core principle behind the entire explanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجمعت أهل السنة على أن الله تعالى متكلم بكلام قديم أزلي. وبرهان العقل فسيأتي. ملاحظة : اعلم أن كلام الله تعالى للنبي موسى عليه السلام أنه أزال منه المانع كلامه القديم الذي ليس بحرف ولا صوت على مذهب الإمام الأشعري وأكثر أهل السنة ، ثم رد عليه المانع فلم يسمع وليس أن الله تعالى ابتدأ كلامه لموسى ولا انْعَدَامَ كلامه بعد رد المانع عليه لأن كلامه تعالى قديم والقديم لا يتغير ولا يتبدل.
And the Ahl al-Sunnah have unanimously agreed that Allah the Exalted is speaking with an eternal, beginningless speech. And the rational proof will come later. Note: Know that Allah the Exalted's speech to the Prophet Moses, peace be upon him—that He removed from him the barrier (al-māni‘) [so that he heard] His eternal speech, which is neither a letter nor a sound, according to the school of Imam al-Ash‘arī and the majority of Ahl al-Sunnah. Then He restored the barrier to him so he did not hear [further]. And it is not that Allah the Exalted initiated His speech for Moses nor that His speech ceased after restoring the barrier upon him, because His speech is eternal, and the eternal does not change nor alter.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the consensus of Ahl al-Sunnah that Allah is described as speaking (mutakallim) with an eternal, beginningless speech (kalām qadīm azalī). The rational proof for this is promised later. Then a note is given to clarify the event of Moses hearing God's speech. The explanation is that God removed a barrier (al-māni‘) from Moses so that he heard God's eternal speech, which according to Ash‘arī doctrine is not composed of letters or sound. After the event, the barrier was restored, so Moses no longer heard it. This is to correct the misunderstanding that God either began His speech for Moses (which would imply temporality) or that His speech ceased after Moses heard it (which would imply change). Since God's speech is eternal, it cannot begin or cease. The eternal does not undergo change or alteration.

Key Terms

المانع
حرف

أهل السنة
الإمام الأشعري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's speech to Moses was a temporal event that started when God spoke and stopped when Moses stopped hearing.
Correction: The passage clarifies that God's speech is eternal and never begins or ceases. Moses heard by removal of a barrier, and stopped hearing when the barrier was restored. No change in God's speech occurred.

Checkpoint

A) Because God's speech ended.
B) Because the barrier was restored.
C) Because Moses lost the ability to hear.
D) Because the speech changed into letters and sounds.

Key Takeaway
Allah's speech is eternal and unchanging; the event of Moses hearing was due to removal of a barrier, not a change in divine speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's speech is eternal and unchanging, and the Qur'an is that speech, then when you read the Qur'an, do you treat it as a timeless address to you right now, or as a historical document? What barrier in your heart prevents you from hearing it as if directed at you today?
?
Moses heard Allah's speech only when the barrier was removed. What barriers—like heedlessness, sin, or preoccupation with the world—might be blocking you from perceiving the truth of Allah's eternal words? Will you sincerely ask Him to remove them?
?
The passage insists that Allah did not initiate speech for Moses nor did it cease after. Do you live as though God's communication with you is intermittent and dependent on your actions, or do you believe He is always speaking through His eternal speech? How would your life change if you truly believed His speech never ends?

Seven Requisites of Attributes of Meanings, Six for Life
Sanusi enumerates the seven requisites of the attributes of meanings and the exception for life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven requisites required for each attribute of meaning, and why the attribute of life is an exception with six.

Core Idea
The attributes of meaning have seven necessary aspects, but life lacks one.

This passage from Sanusi's text is a classification of the necessary characteristics that every attribute of meaning must possess. These requisites ensure that the attributes are properly understood as eternal, distinct from the essence, self-sufficient, and universally related. Interestingly, the attribute of life is an exception, having only six of these requisites. Let's explore each one and understand why life is different.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic sentence once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall structure.
Notice the list format and the exception of life.
2
Identify the seven requisites
The passage lists seven attributes: existence, beginninglessness, everlastingness, difference from essence, self-sufficiency, oneness, and generality of relation. Count them and understand each term.
The last one is missing for life.
3
Examine the exception for life
Life is said to have only six requisites, lacking 'generality of relation'. Ask yourself: why might life not relate generically to everything? Think about what life relates to.
Life only relates to the living being, not to all things.
4
Connect to broader doctrine
Reflect on how this classification helps in understanding the divine attributes. It ensures each attribute is properly defined and not confused with others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ملاحظة : صفات المعاني لها مطالب سبعة ، وجودها وقدمها وبقاؤها ومخالفتها وغناؤها عن المخصص ووحدتها وعموم تعلقها بما تتعلق به إلا الحياة فمطالبها ستة.
Note: The attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-ma‘ānī) have seven requisites (maṭālib): their existence, their beginninglessness (qidam), their everlastingness (baqā’), their being different from the essence (mukhālafatuhā), their self-sufficiency from a designer (ghinā’uhā ‘an al-mukhaṣṣiṣ), their oneness (waḥdatuhā), and the generality of their relation (umūm ta‘alluqihā) to everything that they relate to—except life, for its requisites are six.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a classification. It states that the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-ma‘ānī) have seven necessary aspects or requisites (maṭālib). These are: (1) their existence (wujūd), (2) their beginninglessness (qidam), (3) their everlastingness (baqā’), (4) their being different from the essence (mukhālafa), meaning they are not identical to God's essence, (5) their self-sufficiency from a designer (ghinā’ ‘an al-mukhaṣṣiṣ), meaning they are not caused by any creator, (6) their oneness (waḥda), meaning each attribute is one and not multiple, and (7) the generality of their relation (umūm ta‘alluq) to everything they relate to, meaning they are not restricted in scope. However, the exception is the attribute of life (al-ḥayāh), which has only six requisites, lacking the last one (generality of relation) because life does not relate to all things—it only relates to the one who is living.

Misconception: One might think the requisites are separate entities added to the attributes, but they are necessary characteristics inherent to each attribute.
Correction: The requisites are not additional attributes; they are essential aspects of each attribute itself.

?

The Attributes of States: Definition and Concomitance
Defines the fourth category of attributes and explains the mutual concomitance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the attributes of states (al-ṣifāt al-ma‘nawiyyah) are, why they are called ma‘nawiyyah, and how they are necessarily concomitant with the attributes of meanings.

Core Idea
Every existential attribute gives rise to a necessary concomitant state.

In this passage, Sanusi introduces the fourth and final category of divine attributes: the attributes of states. These are not independent qualities but necessary concomitants of the attributes of meanings (such as power, will, and knowledge). Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the full spectrum of attributes that theology affirms for God.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic definition of the fourth category. Note the term 'ṣifāt ma‘nawiyyah' and why they are named that way.
The name indicates their relation to the meanings (ma‘ānī).
2
Understand the concomitance
The passage emphasises that the concomitance is mutual: if the meaning attribute exists, the state attribute necessarily exists, and vice versa. Consider the example of power and being powerful.
This is not a causal relationship but a logical necessity.
3
Grasp the reasoning
Read the explanation: every existential attribute (like power) subsisting in a subject necessitates a state (like being powerful) that does not obtain without that attribute. This applies to all seven meaning attributes.
The seven states correspond to the seven meaning attributes.
4
Apply to other attributes
Analogize the reasoning to the remaining attributes: will, knowledge, life, hearing, sight, and speech. Each produces a corresponding state.
5
Reflect on the footnote
The footnote from al-Bayjūrī reiterates the mutuality of concomitance. Consider why this point is important for avoiding misunderstandings.
Some might think the relation is one-way, but it is mutual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الرابع الصفات المعنوية. وسميت معنوية لأنها منسوبة للمعاني بمعنى أنها ملازمة لها (١). واعلم أن الملازمة من الطرفين). (١) كونه تعالى قادراً لازم للقدرة، وكونه مريداً لازم للإرادة القائمة بذاته تعالى، وهكذا الخمسة الباقية، ووجه لزوم هذه الأكوان السبعة للمعاني أن كلاً من المعاني صفة وجودية وكل صفة وجودية إذا قامت بموصوف قديم أو حادث لزم أن يكتسب منها حالاً لا تثبت له عند عدم تلك الصفة منه، فمن قامت به القدرة لزم أن يكتسب حالاً وهي أن يكون قادراً على ما تعلقت به تلك القدرة، ويعبر عن هذه الحال بكونه قادراً وبالقادِريَّة. ومن قام به العلم لزم أن يكتسب منه حالاً وهي أن يكون عالماً بما تعلق به هذا العلم ومطلعا عليه، ويعبر عن هذه الحال بكونه عالماً وبالعالمية وقس الباقي.(غ). (٢) هذا - أي كون التلازم من الطرفين - نبه إليه البيجوري في حاشيته على أم البراهين (س).
The fourth category: the attributes of states (al-ṣifāt al-ma‘nawiyyah). They are called ma‘nawiyyah because they are relative to the meanings (al-ma‘ānī) in the sense that they are concomitant (mulāzima) with them. And know that the concomitance is mutual (min al-ṭarafayn). (1) His being (kawnuhu) powerful is necessary (lāzim) for power, His being willing is necessary for the will subsisting in His essence, and similarly the remaining five. And the reason for the necessity of these seven states (akwān) for the meanings is that each of the meanings is an existential attribute (ṣifa wujūdiyya), and every existential attribute when it subsists in a described being (mawṣūf), whether eternal or contingent, necessarily entails that it acquires a state (ḥāl) that does not obtain for it in the absence of that attribute. So whoever has power subsisting in him necessarily acquires a state, namely that he is powerful over that which the power relates to; this state is expressed as 'being powerful' (kawnuhu qādiran) and as 'powerfulness' (al-qādariyya). And whoever has knowledge subsisting in him necessarily acquires from it a state, namely that he is knowledgeable of that which this knowledge relates to and informed of it; this state is expressed as 'being knowledgeable' (kawnuhu ‘āliman) and 'knowledgeableness' (al-‘ālimiyya). And analogize the rest. (Gh). (2) This—i.e., the mutuality of concomitance—was pointed out by al-Bayjūrī in his gloss on Umm al-Barāhīn. (S).
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth category of divine attributes: the attributes of states (al-ṣifāt al-ma‘nawiyyah). These are called 'ma‘nawiyyah' because they are relative to the attributes of meanings (al-ṣifāt al-ma‘āniyya), meaning they are concomitant (lāzima) with them. The author emphasises that this concomitance is mutual (min al-ṭarafayn): if the attribute of meaning exists, the attribute of state necessarily follows, and vice versa. The footnote elaborates: for example, God's being powerful (kawnuhu qādiran) is necessary for the attribute of power (qudra) in His essence. Similarly for willing, knowing, living, hearing, seeing, and speaking. The reason given is that each attribute of meaning is an existential quality; whenever such a quality subsists in a subject, it necessarily produces a state (ḥāl) that does not exist without it. For instance, when power subsists, the subject necessarily acquires the state of being powerful over that which the power relates to. This state is referred to as 'being powerful' or 'powerfulness'. The same applies to knowledge and the others. The second footnote notes that al-Bayjūrī pointed out the mutuality of concomitance in his gloss.

Key Terms
الصفات المعنوية
المعاني
ملازمة
كون
حال
قادريَّة
عالمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the attributes of states are separate, independent entities.
Correction: They are merely necessary concomitants of the existential attributes of meanings, not independent qualities.

Checkpoint
Why are these attributes called 'ma‘nawiyyah'?
A) Because they are independent of the meanings.
B) Because they are relative to the meanings and concomitant with them.
C) Because they are mental concepts only.
D) Because they are eternal in themselves.

Key Takeaway
The attributes of states are necessary concomitants of the existential meaning attributes, and the relationship is mutual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's being powerful is a necessary concomitant of His power, can you truly trust that He is powerful over every detail of your life? What area of your life feels beyond His control, and how does this logical necessity challenge your doubt? Will you consciously affirm His power in that area today?
?
The author says the state (ḥāl) does not exist without the attribute. When you reflect on God's knowledge (ʿilm) and His being knowledgeable (ʿālim), does it change how you live knowing that every action of yours is fully known and witnessed? Is there a sin you commit as if God is not aware? What would it mean for you to truly internalize that His knowledge entails His being fully aware of you?
?
This mutual concomitance means that God's attributes are not arbitrary but necessary. Does your worship reflect the certainty that God's attributes are perfectly coherent and necessary? Or do you sometimes act as if God could be one way in one situation and another in the next? How can you cultivate a deeper reverence knowing that His attributes are absolutely consistent and necessarily entailed by His essence?

Divine Attributes
beginner
10 min
The Seven Attributes of States
Sanusi enumerates and defines the seven attributes of states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven attributes of states (al-ṣifāt al-ma‘nawiyyah) and how each is a necessary state (ḥāl) resulting from a corresponding attribute of meaning rooted in the divine essence.

Core Idea
The seven states are necessary concomitants of the seven attributes of meaning.

In this passage, Sanusi enumerates the seven attributes of states (al-ṣifāt al-ma‘nawiyyah) that necessarily inhere in the divine essence. Each is defined as a state (ḥāl) that follows from the presence of a corresponding attribute of meaning—power, will, knowledge, life, hearing, sight, and speech. Understanding these states helps clarify the relationship between Allah's essential attributes and His actual descriptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pattern
Notice that each attribute is introduced with 'kawnuhu' (His being) and explained as a necessary state due to the subsistence of the corresponding attribute.
2
Study the definitions
For each of the seven, read the definition of the reality (ḥaqīqah) of that state. Note how each is grounded in a specific attribute of meaning.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why these states are considered necessary and how they relate to the divine essence without being separate eternal entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصفات المعنوية هي
كونه قادراً : والكون هو الثبوت وحقيقة القادر هو المتمكن من الفعل والترك. كونه مريدا وهي حال واجبة للذات لما قامت الإرادة بذات الله تعالى وجب وصفه بكونه مريداً. وحقيقة المريد هو الذي يرجح أحد طرفي الممكن على الطرف الآخر. كونه عالماً : هي حال واجب للذات ، لما قام العلم بذات الله تعالى وجب وصفه بكونه وحقيقة العالم هو الذي انكشف لعلمه الواجب والمستحيل والجائز. كونه حياً : هي حال واجبة للذات. لما قامت الحياة بذات الله وجب وصفه بكونه حياً. وحقيقة الحي هو الذي صح وصفه بصفات الإدراك.
كونه سميعاً : هي حال واجبة للذات، لما قام السمع بذات الله وجب وصفه بكونه سميعا. وحقيقة السميع هو الذي انكشف لسمعه كل موجود.
كونه بصيراً : هي حال واجبة للذات، لما قام البصر بذات الله وجب وصفه بكونه بصيرا، وحقيقة البصر هو الذي انكشف البصره كل موجود.
كونه متكلماً : هي حال واجبة للذات ، لما قام الكلام بذات الله وجب وصفه بكونه متكلماً، وحقيقة المتكلم الذي دل كلامه على الواجب والمستحيل والجائز.
And the attributes of states are: His being powerful (kawnuhu qādiran): and al-kawn is existence (al-thubūt), and the reality of the powerful (al-qādir) is the one who is capable (al-mutamakkin) of action and inaction. His being willing (kawnuhu murīdan): it is a state (ḥāl) necessary for the essence; since will (al-irāda) subsists in the essence of Allah the Exalted, it is necessary to describe Him as being willing. And the reality of the willing (al-murīd) is the one who gives preponderance (yurajjiḥ) to one of the two sides of a possible thing (al-mumkin) over the other side. His being knowing (kawnuhu ‘āliman): it is a state necessary for the essence; since knowledge (al-‘ilm) subsists in the essence of Allah the Exalted, it is necessary to describe Him as being knowing (kawnuhu ‘āliman). And the reality of the knowing (al-‘ālim) is the one whose knowledge reveals (inkasha‘a) the necessary, the impossible, and the possible. His being living (kawnuhu ḥayyan): it is a state necessary for the essence; since life (al-ḥayāh) subsists in the essence of Allah, it is necessary to describe Him as being living. And the reality of the living (al-ḥayy) is the one who is correctly described with the attributes of perception (al-idrāk). His being hearing (kawnuhu samī‘an): it is a state necessary for the essence; since hearing (al-sam‘) subsists in the essence of Allah, it is necessary to describe Him as being hearing. And the reality of the hearing (al-samī‘) is the one whose hearing reveals every existing thing. His being seeing (kawnuhu baṣīran): it is a state necessary for the essence; since sight (al-baṣar) subsists in the essence of Allah, it is necessary to describe Him as being seeing. And the reality of the seeing (al-baṣīr) is the one whose sight reveals every existing thing. His being speaking (kawnuhu mutakalliman): it is a state necessary for the essence; since speech (al-kalām) subsists in the essence of Allah, it is necessary to describe Him as being speaking. And the reality of the speaking (al-mutakallim) is the one whose speech indicates (dalla) the necessary, the impossible, and the possible.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage enumerates and defines the seven attributes of states (al-ṣifāt al-ma‘nawiyyah). Each is introduced with the phrase 'kawnuhu...' (His being...), indicating that it is a state (ḥāl) necessarily subsisting in the essence due to the corresponding attribute of meaning. The first is His being powerful (kawnuhu qādiran): 'al-kawn' means existence (thubūt), and the reality of 'powerful' is one who has the ability (tamakkun) to act or refrain from acting. The second is His being willing (kawnuhu murīdan): a state necessitated by will (irāda) subsisting in His essence; the willing is the one who gives preponderance to one of two possible outcomes. The third is His being knowing (kawnuhu ‘āliman): necessitated by knowledge (‘ilm) subsisting in His essence; the knowing is one whose knowledge unveils the necessary (wājib), impossible (mustaḥīl), and possible (jā’iz). The fourth is His being living (kawnuhu ḥayyan): necessitated by life (ḥayāh) subsisting in His essence; the living is one validly described with attributes of perception (idrāk). The fifth is His being hearing (kawnuhu samī‘an): necessitated by hearing (sam‘); the hearing is one whose hearing reveals every existent. The sixth is His being seeing (kawnuhu baṣīran): necessitated by sight (baṣar); the seeing is one whose sight reveals every existent. The seventh is His being speaking (kawnuhu mutakalliman): necessitated by speech (kalām); the speaking is one whose speech indicates the necessary, impossible, and possible.

Key Terms
كون
قادر
مريد
عالم
حي
سميع
بصير
متكلم
حال
ثبوت
تمكن
ترجيح
انكشاف
إدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These seven states are independent eternal attributes existing alongside the attributes of meaning.
Correction: They are not independent; they are necessary concomitant states (aḥwāl) that necessarily follow from the subsistence of the attributes of meaning in the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the reality of 'the hearing' (al-samī‘)?
A) The one who hears sounds.
B) The one whose hearing reveals every existing thing.
C) The one who has ears.
D) The one who can speak.

Key Takeaway
The seven attributes of states are necessary concomitants of the seven attributes of meaning, and each is defined by its unique reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that Allah's hearing (sam‘) reveals every existing thing. Every word you have ever spoken, every thought you have ever entertained, is fully exposed to Him. Is there a conversation you are planning that you would be ashamed for Him to hear? Will you cancel it, or will you speak as if He is not listening?
?
The reality of Allah's power (qudra) is that He is truly capable of action and inaction—nothing constrains Him. You, on the other hand, are constrained by your abilities, your resources, your very body. When you fail to achieve something you desperately want, do you rage against God as if He owes you success? Or do you submit to His absolute power, acknowledging that every outcome is His alone to give?

Definition of the Seven Moral Attributes
Al-Sanusi enumerates the seven moral attributes and defines each one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the seven moral attributes are, why they are called 'moral', and how each is defined in relation to its corresponding attribute of meaning.

Core Idea
The seven moral attributes are necessary states that arise from the seven attributes of meaning.

Al-Sanusi now introduces the seven moral attributes, which are the states (أحوال) that necessarily accompany the seven attributes of meaning. Before diving into each, he explains why they are called 'معنوية' (moral) — because they are attributed to the meanings (المعاني) and are inseparable from them. This passage gives a concise definition of each moral attribute, linking it to its corresponding attribute of meaning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the term 'معنوية'
Read the opening sentence explaining why they are called moral attributes. Note that the inseparability is mutual.
2
Notice the pattern
Each moral attribute follows the same structure: 'كونه [attribute]: it is a state necessary for the Essence when [corresponding attribute] inheres...'
3
Examine each definition
For each of the seven, note the reality (حقيقة) of the attribute as defined by Al-Sanusi.
4
Reflect on the mutual inseparability
The passage says the inseparability is from both sides — meaning the moral attributes and the attributes of meaning are always together.
5
Test your understanding
Use the checkpoint to confirm you can distinguish the definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسميت معنوية لأنها منسوبة للمعاني بمعنى أنها ملازمة لها. واعلم أن الملازمة من الطرفين. والصفات المعنوية هي: كونه قادراً: والكون هو الثبوت وحقيقة القادر هو المتمكن من الفعل والترك. كونه مريدا: وهي حال واجبة للذات لما قامت الإرادة بذات الله تعالى وجب وصفه بكونه مريداً. وحقيقة المريد هو الذي يرجح أحد طرفي الممكن على الطرف الآخر. كونه عالماً: هي حال واجب للذات، لما قام العلم بذات الله تعالى وجب وصفه بكونه عالماً. وحقيقة العالم هو الذي انكشف لعلمه الواجب والمستحيل والجائز. كونه حياً: هي حال واجبة للذات. لما قامت الحياة بذات الله وجب وصفه بكونه حياً. وحقيقة الحي هو الذي صح وصفه بصفات الإدراك. كونه سميعاً: هي حال واجبة للذات، لما قام السمع بذات الله وجب وصفه بكونه سميعا. وحقيقة السميع هو الذي انكشف لسمعه كل موجود. كونه بصيراً: هي حال واجبة للذات، لما قام البصر بذات الله وجب وصفه بكونه بصيرا، وحقيقة البصر هو الذي انكشف البصره كل موجود. كونه متكلماً: هي حال واجبة للذات، لما قام الكلام بذات الله وجب وصفه بكونه متكلماً، وحقيقة المتكلم الذي دل كلامه على الواجب والمستحيل والجائز.
They are called 'moral' (معنوية) because they are attributed to the meanings (معان) in the sense that they are inseparable from them. Know that the inseparability is from both sides. The moral attributes are: His being powerful: 'being' (كون) is existence, and the reality of the powerful is the one capable of action and inaction. His being willing: it is a state necessary for the Essence; when will (إرادة) inheres in the Essence of Allah, it becomes necessary to describe Him as being willing. And the reality of the willing is the one who gives preference to one of the two possibilities over the other. His being knowing: it is a state necessary for the Essence; when knowledge (علم) inheres in the Essence of Allah, it becomes necessary to describe Him as being knowing. And the reality of the knowing is the one to whom the necessary, impossible, and possible are disclosed through His knowledge. His being living: it is a state necessary for the Essence; when life (حياة) inheres in the Essence of Allah, it becomes necessary to describe Him as being living. And the reality of the living is the one for whom it is correct to be described with attributes of perception. His being hearing: it is a state necessary for the Essence; when hearing (سمع) inheres in the Essence of Allah, it becomes necessary to describe Him as being hearing. And the reality of the hearing is the one to whom every existent is disclosed through His hearing. His being seeing: it is a state necessary for the Essence; when sight (بصر) inheres in the Essence of Allah, it becomes necessary to describe Him as being seeing. And the reality of the seeing is the one to whom every existent is disclosed through His sight. His being speaking: it is a state necessary for the Essence; when speech (كلام) inheres in the Essence of Allah, it becomes necessary to describe Him as being speaking. And the reality of the speaking is the one whose speech indicates the necessary, impossible, and possible.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage defines the seven moral attributes (الصفات المعنوية) which are states (أحوال) that inhere in the Divine Essence concomitantly with the seven attributes of meaning (الصفات المعاني). The author first explains why they are called 'معنوية': they are attributed to the 'معان' (meaning-attributions) because they are inseparable from them; the inseparability is mutual (from both sides). Then he enumerates each attribute: (1) His being powerful (كونه قادراً): 'كون' means 'being' or 'existence', and the powerful one is capable of both act and refrain. (2) His being willing (كونه مريداً): a necessary state arising from the attribute of will (إرادة) inhering in the Essence; the willing one is He who gives preference to one side of possibility over the other. (3) His being knowing (كونه عالماً): a necessary state from knowledge (علم); the knowing one is He to whom the necessary, impossible, and possible are disclosed. (4) His being living (كونه حياً): a necessary state from life (حياة); the living one is He who can be described with perceptive attributes. (5) His being hearing (كونه سميعاً): a necessary state from hearing (سمع); the hearing one is He to whom every existent is disclosed via His hearing. (6) His being seeing (كونه بصيراً): a necessary state from sight (بصر); the seeing one is He to whom every existent is disclosed via His sight. (7) His being speaking (كونه متكلماً): a necessary state from speech (كلام); the speaking one is He whose speech indicates the necessary, impossible, and possible. Each definition stresses that the attribute is a 'حال واجبة للذات' (a state necessary for the Essence) that arises when the corresponding صفة معنى inheres in the Essence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: الصفات المعنوية are the same as الصفات المعاني
Correction: They are distinct states (أحوال) that necessarily accompany the attributes of meaning; they are inseparable but not identical.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is defined as 'the one who gives preference to one of the two possibilities over the other'?
A) القادر (al-Qādir)
B) المريد (al-Murīd)
C) العالم (al-ʿĀlim)
D) المتكلم (al-Mutakallim)

Key Takeaway
The seven moral attributes are necessary states that arise from the seven attributes of meaning, each defined by its specific relation to divine action, will, knowledge, life, hearing, sight, and speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that Allah's being powerful means He is truly capable of all action and inaction, in what specific area of your life are you still trying to control outcomes yourself instead of trusting His power? What step will you take this week to surrender that area to Him?
?
The attribute of being willing (مريد) means Allah gives preference to one possibility over another. Think of a disappointment or trial you are facing. How does knowing that Allah willed that specific outcome change your perspective? Will you accept His choice with contentment, or do you still harbor resentment?
?
Allah's being knowing (عالم) means He sees the necessary, impossible, and possible. What 'impossible' situation in your life—be it a broken relationship, a financial crisis, or a personal weakness—do you need to entrust to His knowledge, believing that He already knows the way through it? Write down one concrete action you will take to rely on His knowledge today.

Divine Attributes

Distinction Between Attributes of Meaning and Moral Attributes
Al-Sanusi distinguishes صفات المعاني from صفات المعنوية and notes the dispute on الحال.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between attributes of meaning (المعاني) and moral attributes (المعنوية), and how the concept of الحال (state) affects the categorization of divine attributes.

Core Idea
Attributes of meaning are real; moral attributes are mental constructs, and the disagreement about الحال changes how many categories there are.

In this lesson, Al-Sanusi clarifies a crucial distinction between two types of divine attributes: those that have external existence (المعاني) and those that are only mentally posited (المعنوية). He then notes the theological dispute over the concept of الحال (state), which directly affects how these attributes are classified. Understanding this distinction is foundational for grasping later debates in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Focus on the main distinction and the three notes.
Notice the technical terms: وجودي, ثبوتي, الحال.
2
Understand the difference between وجودي and ثبوتي
Attributes of meaning (المعاني) are existential (وجودية) - they exist both in the mind and externally. Moral attributes (المعنوية) are positive (ثبوتية) - they exist only in the mind, as mental concepts.
This is a key ontological distinction.
3
Grasp the dispute on الحال
Note that al-Qāḍī al-Bāqillānī and his followers affirm the concept of 'state' (الحال) as an intermediate entity between existence and non-existence. Al-Ashʿarī and the majority reject this, reducing moral attributes to the inherence of meanings in the Essence.
The dispute affects how many intelligibles are recognized.
4
Count the intelligibles for each view
Affirmers of الحال have four intelligibles: Essence, meanings, moral states, and names. Deniers have three: Essence, meanings, and names. Use the passage to verify.
This summarizes the impact of the dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفرق بين المعاني والمعنوية أن المعاني وجودية تعقل ذهناً وخارجاً، والمعنوية ثبوتية تعقل ذهناً لا خارجاً). ملاحظة : الصفات المعنوية قال بها من أثبت الحال وهو القاضي الباقلاني ومن وافقه ، وقال الإمام الأشعري والجمهور بنفي الحال والمعنوية عندهم راجعة للمعاني ، وهي عبارة عن قيام المعاني بالذات. ملاحظة : إذا قلنا كونه مريداً، كونه قادراً .... إلخ، فهي صفات معنوية، وأما بدون كلمة كونه فتصبح أسماء للذات مثل مريد، قادر ...... إلخ. ملاحظة : إن من أثبت الحال عنده أربعة معقولات : الذات والمعاني والأحوال المعنوية الملازمة لها والأسماء ومن نفى الحال عنده ثلاثة معقولات : الذات والمعاني والأسماء
And the difference between the meanings (المعاني) and the moral (المعنوية) is that the meanings are existential (وجودية), conceivable mentally and externally, while the moral are positive (ثبوتية), conceivable mentally not externally. Note: The moral attributes are affirmed by those who affirm the state (الحال), which is al-Qāḍī al-Bāqillānī and those who agree with him, while al-Imām al-Ashʿarī and the majority deny the state, and the moral for them refer back to the meanings, and they are the expression of the inherence of the meanings in the Essence. Note: When we say 'His being willing', 'His being powerful', etc., these are moral attributes, but without the word 'being' (كونه) they become names for the Essence, such as 'Willing', 'Powerful', etc. Note: Whoever affirms the state has four intelligibles: the Essence (الذات), the meanings (المعاني), the moral states (الأحوال المعنوية) concomitant to them, and the names (الأسماء). And whoever denies the state has three intelligibles: the Essence, the meanings, and the names.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage first distinguishes between attributes of meaning (صفات المعاني) and moral attributes (الصفات المعنوية). Attributes of meaning are existential (وجودية), meaning they have concrete existence both in the mind and externally; moral attributes are positive (ثبوتية) and are only conceivable in the mind, not externally. Then the author provides three notes. Note 1: The moral attributes are affirmed by those who accept the concept of 'state' (الحال), namely al-Qāḍī al-Bāqillānī and his followers, while al-Imām al-Ashʿarī and the majority reject the state, and for them the moral attributes revert to the meanings, i.e., they are simply the inherence of the meanings in the Essence. Note 2: When we say 'كونه مريداً' (His being willing), this is a moral attribute; without the word 'كونه' it becomes a name of the Essence, e.g., 'مريد' (Willing). Note 3: The proponent of the state has four intelligibles: the Essence, the meanings, the concomitant moral states, and the names. The denier of the state has only three: the Essence, the meanings, and the names. Thus the affirmation or denial of الحال directly impacts how many distinct categories of attributes are recognized.

المعنوية

القاضي الباقلاني
الإمام الأشعري
الجمهور
معقولات

الأسماء
الأحوال المعنوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that الصفات المعنوية are real external entities like the attributes of meaning.
Correction: The passage clarifies that moral attributes are only mentally conceivable (ثبوتية تعقل ذهناً لا خارجاً), so they are not external realities.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many intelligibles does a denier of الحال have?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The distinction between الصفات المعاني (real) and الصفات المعنوية (mental) depends on the concept of الحال, which determines whether moral attributes are considered separate categories or not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you refer to Allah as 'Willing' or 'Powerful' (omitting the word 'being'), do you ever slip into thinking these are mere labels rather than real attributes? How does the distinction between names and moral attributes sharpen your awareness of His perfections?
?
The scholars disagreed on whether moral attributes are separate from the attributes of meaning. How does your own intellectual posture toward theological nuance affect your trust in what you believe? Are you willing to engage with disagreements, or do you prefer to simplify?
?
Al-Bāqillānī saw the moral states as real mental entities. In your worship, do you feel the reality of Allah's attributes as distinct from His essence? How might affirming (or denying) the state change how you internalize His attributes in prayer and reflection?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
